  "content": "# client-go: Advanced Systems Guide\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies\n\n`client-go` is the official Go client library for the Kubernetes API. It is the canonical package for anything that communicates with kube-apiserver: controllers in the control plane, CLI tools like `kubectl`, admission webhooks, and any external operator. Almost every component in the Kubernetes ecosystem is, directly or transitively, a client-go user.\n\n**Canonical location:** `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/` (published externally as `k8s.io/client-go`).\n\nThe library defines a strict version relationship: `client-go v0.X.Y` corresponds to Kubernetes `v1.X.Y`. A client built against an older version is guaranteed to work against a newer API server (forward compatibility), but the reverse is not guaranteed (see `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md`).\n\n### Upstream dependencies\n\n- `k8s.io/apimachinery` \u2014 provides the runtime object model, serialization, watch event types, and the `StatusError` type used for structured error handling.\n- `k8s.io/api` \u2014 contains the generated Go types for all built-in API groups.\n- `k8s.io/code-generator` \u2014 generates the typed clientsets, informers, listers, and apply-configuration builders that make up the majority of the code in `kubernetes/`, `informers/`, `listers/`, and `applyconfigurations/`.\n\n### Downstream consumers\n\n- **kube-controller-manager / cloud-controller-manager** \u2014 every built-in controller uses the `SharedInformerFactory`, typed clientsets, and the work-queue pattern.\n- **kube-scheduler** \u2014 uses informers for its cache of pods, nodes, and other scheduling-relevant objects.\n- **`kubectl`** \u2014 uses `tools/clientcmd` for kubeconfig loading and the `dynamic` client for generic resource manipulation.\n- **External operators and CRD controllers** \u2014 depend on `client-go` directly, typically via the controller-runtime abstraction built on top of it.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and Their Responsibilities\n\n### 2.1 Configuration Layer: `rest.Config` and `tools/clientcmd`\n\n`rest.Config` (`rest/config.go`) is the central in-memory representation of connection and authentication parameters. Its critical fields are:\n\n| Field | Purpose |\n|---|---|\n| `Host` | Base URL of the API server |\n| `BearerToken` / `BearerTokenFile` | Static token or auto-refreshed file |\n| `TLSClientConfig` | CA bundle, client cert/key |\n| `QPS`, `Burst` | Client-side token-bucket rate limiting |\n| `RateLimiter` | Override for `QPS`/`Burst` with a custom `flowcontrol.RateLimiter` |\n| `Timeout` | Per-request timeout |\n\nDefault `QPS` is 5.0 and default `Burst` is 10 (`rest/config.go:47-48`). These are deliberately conservative; production controllers frequently need higher values.\n\n`InClusterConfig()` (`rest/config.go:543`) reads `KUBERNETES_SERVICE_HOST`/`KUBERNETES_SERVICE_PORT` and the projected service-account token from `/var/run/secrets/kubernetes.io/serviceaccount/`. This is the canonical path for pods authenticating to the API server.\n\n`tools/clientcmd` handles kubeconfig parsing, context merging, and external credential provider discovery. The result of `clientcmd.BuildConfigFromFlags()` is always a `rest.Config`.\n\n### 2.2 Transport Layer: `transport/`\n\nThe transport layer translates a `rest.Config` into an `http.RoundTripper` chain. The function `HTTPWrappersForConfig()` (`transport/round_trippers.go:41`) assembles the chain in this order:\n\n```\nUser-provided WrapTransport\n  \u2192 DebugWrappers (if log level \u2265 6)\n  \u2192 BearerAuthWithRefreshRoundTripper (token/token-file auth)\n  \u2192 BasicAuthRoundTripper\n  \u2192 UserAgentRoundTripper\n  \u2192 ImpersonatingRoundTripper\n  \u2192 (TLS base transport)\n```\n\nEach element implements `http.RoundTripper` and wraps the next via a well-defined decorator pattern. This means authentication is injected purely at the transport level; the higher-level request builders have no knowledge of credential specifics.\n\n#### Exec credential plugins (`plugin/pkg/client/auth/exec/exec.go`)\n\nThe exec plugin mechanism (`Authenticator.UpdateTransportConfig`, line 312) registers an additional `RoundTripper` that, on each request, checks whether the cached `credentials` struct is still valid (`credsExpired()`, line 386). If not, it spawns the configured external binary (`exec.Command`, line 457), encodes the current cluster info into `KUBERNETES_EXEC_INFO`, and parses the returned `ExecCredential` object. The caching layer (`cache`, line 97) ensures that multiple clientsets using the same exec configuration share a single `Authenticator` instance, preventing redundant process spawns.\n\n#### Token file rotation (`transport/token_source.go`)\n\n`BearerTokenFile` is implemented via a background goroutine that periodically re-reads the file on disk. This is the mechanism behind projected service-account token rotation in pods \u2014 the kubelet rotates the token on disk, and the client picks it up without restart.\n\n### 2.3 REST Client Layer: `rest/`\n\n`RESTClient` (`rest/client.go:86`) is the foundational HTTP client. Its `Interface` (`rest/client.go:46`) exposes a builder pattern:\n\n```go\nclient.Get().\n    Namespace(\"default\").\n    Resource(\"pods\").\n    Name(\"foo\").\n    Do(ctx).\n    Into(&pod)\n```\n\nThe `Request` type (`rest/request.go`) accumulates verb, resource, namespace, name, query parameters, and body. No I/O occurs until a terminal method is called:\n\n- `Do(ctx)` \u2014 performs the HTTP call and returns a `Result`; `Result.Into(&obj)` deserializes the response.\n- `Watch(ctx)` \u2014 opens a persistent streaming connection and returns a `watch.Interface` providing a channel of `watch.Event`.\n- `Stream(ctx)` \u2014 returns a raw `io.ReadCloser`, used for log streaming and exec.\n\nContent negotiation is driven by `AcceptContentTypes` in `ClientContentConfig`. By default the client negotiates JSON; Protobuf is preferred for built-in types by specifying `application/vnd.kubernetes.protobuf`. The alpha `ClientsAllowCBOR`/`ClientsPreferCBOR` feature gates (`features/known_features.go:49-60`) extend this to CBOR.\n\nServer-side throttling: when the API server returns `HTTP 429`, the client's default retry logic reads the `Retry-After` header and waits before re-issuing the request.\n\n### 2.4 Typed Clientset: `kubernetes/`\n\n`kubernetes.Clientset` (`kubernetes/clientset.go`) is a generated composite type that embeds one typed client per API group (e.g., `CoreV1()`, `AppsV1()`, `BatchV1()`). Each group client is built from a shared `rest.Interface`. This provides compile-time type safety for all built-in API operations.\n\nUsage pattern:\n```go\ncs, _ := kubernetes.NewForConfig(cfg)\npod, _ := cs.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").Get(ctx, \"foo\", metav1.GetOptions{})\n```\n\nThe `kubernetes_test/` directory contains integration-style tests that verify the generated code compiles and basic patterns work.\n\n### 2.5 Dynamic Client: `dynamic/`\n\n`DynamicClient` (`dynamic/simple.go:33`) operates on `*unstructured.Unstructured` objects and accepts a `schema.GroupVersionResource` at runtime rather than compile time. The `ResourceInterface` (`dynamic/interface.go:33`) exposes CRUD, Watch, Patch, and Apply operations.\n\nThis client's `ConfigFor()` function (`dynamic/simple.go:42`) always sets `ContentType` and `AcceptContentTypes` to `application/json` (or CBOR if feature gates allow), because the dynamic client has no typed codec for protobuf negotiation.\n\nThe dynamic client is the natural substrate for CRD controllers, admission webhooks that must handle arbitrary resource types, and tools like `kubectl apply`.\n\n### 2.6 Discovery Client: `discovery/`\n\n`DiscoveryClient` (`discovery/discovery_client.go:164`) queries `/api`, `/apis`, and individual group endpoints to map what GroupVersionResources the server supports. It implements `DiscoveryInterface` which aggregates:\n\n- `ServerGroupsInterface` \u2014 lists API groups\n- `ServerResourcesInterface` \u2014 lists resources per GVK\n- `OpenAPISchemaInterface` / `OpenAPIV3SchemaInterface` \u2014 fetches schema documents\n\nThe `CachedDiscoveryInterface` wraps a `DiscoveryClient` with disk caching to avoid repeated expensive discovery calls, especially important for `kubectl` which performs discovery on every invocation.\n\nAggregated discovery (v2/v2beta1) allows the API server to return all groups and their resources in a single round-trip using the Accept header `g=apidiscovery.k8s.io;v=v2;as=APIGroupDiscoveryList` (`discovery/discovery_client.go:63-68`).\n\n### 2.7 Metadata Client: `metadata/`\n\n`metadata.Client` (`metadata/metadata.go:57`) is a specialized client that requests `PartialObjectMetadata` \u2014 only the object metadata (name, namespace, labels, annotations, owner references, resource version) without the spec/status payload. This is crucial for garbage collection, namespace controllers, and other controllers that need broad list/watch coverage across resource types but do not need full object bodies. It uses the Accept header `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1`.\n\n### 2.8 Server-Side Apply: `applyconfigurations/`\n\nThe `applyconfigurations/` package contains generated type-safe builder structs (e.g., `PodApplyConfiguration`, `applyconfigurations/core/v1/pod.go:35`) for constructing declarative patch payloads. These are used with the typed client's `Apply()` method:\n\n```go\npod := corev1apply.Pod(\"foo\", \"default\").\n    WithSpec(corev1apply.PodSpec().WithContainers(...))\ncs.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").Apply(ctx, pod, metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: \"my-controller\"})\n```\n\n`ExtractPodFrom()` (`applyconfigurations/core/v1/pod.go:69`) extracts only the fields owned by a given field manager from a live object, enabling the safe \"extract-modify-apply\" pattern that avoids overwriting fields owned by other actors. This is architecturally distinct from the get-modify-update pattern which risks conflicts under concurrent ownership.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Controller Infrastructure and End-to-End Flow\n\nThe controller pattern in client-go is built around three interacting components: the **Reflector**, the **DeltaFIFO**, and the **SharedIndexInformer**. Together they replace a polling model with a push-driven, eventually-consistent local cache.\n\n### 3.1 Reflector (`tools/cache/reflector.go`)\n\nThe `Reflector` struct (`reflector.go:80`) owns the LIST/WATCH loop against the API server. Its public entry point is `RunWithContext()` (`reflector.go:364`), which calls `ListAndWatchWithContext()` in a loop managed by `wait.BackoffUntil`:\n\n```\nRunWithContext()\n  \u2514\u2500 BackoffUntil(ListAndWatchWithContext)\n       \u251c\u2500 watchList (if WatchListClient feature enabled)  // streaming initial list\n       \u2514\u2500 list()                                           // paginated LIST fallback\n            \u2514\u2500 watchWithResync()\n                 \u251c\u2500 startResync() [goroutine]              // periodic resync timer\n                 \u2514\u2500 watch()                                // streaming WATCH loop\n```\n\n**Initial synchronization:** `list()` (`reflector.go:612`) uses `tools/pager.ListPager` with a default page size of 500 (`tools/pager/pager.go:31`) to chunk large resource lists. This prevents a single enormous API response from overwhelming memory. The resulting `resourceVersion` anchors the subsequent watch.\n\n**WatchListClient feature gate:** When the `WatchListClient` feature gate is enabled (GA at v1.35, `features/known_features.go:120-123`), the reflector uses a streaming watch-based initial sync (`r.watchList(ctx)`) instead of a separate LIST call. The server sends all matching objects as `ADDED` events followed by a synthetic `BOOKMARK` event that signals the initial list is complete. This reduces the number of round-trips and peak memory usage during large relists.\n\n**Watch loop:** `watch()` (`reflector.go:499`) issues a `WatchWithContext()` call with a randomized timeout between `minWatchTimeout` and `2*minWatchTimeout` (default 5\u201310 minutes) to spread reconnect load across API server instances. It requests `AllowWatchBookmarks: true` to receive `BOOKMARK` events \u2014 periodic heartbeats carrying a current `resourceVersion` that allow the reflector to restart from a recent position after a disconnect, rather than falling back to `resourceVersion: \"\"`.\n\n**Error handling:** Watch errors that indicate the watch event buffer overflowed (`410 Gone` / \"too old resource version\") trigger a full relist via `BackoffUntil`. Internal errors (5xx) are retried with `MaxInternalErrorRetryDuration`. The `WatchErrorHandlerWithContext` hook (`reflector.go:189`) allows callers to observe or replace the default error logging behavior (`DefaultWatchErrorHandler`, line 191).\n\n### 3.2 DeltaFIFO (`tools/cache/delta_fifo.go`)\n\n`DeltaFIFO` is the hand-off point between the Reflector and the informer's processing loop. It is a producer-consumer queue where:\n\n- The key for each object is computed by `KeyFunc` (default: `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc`, producing `namespace/name` or just `name`).\n- Each entry in the queue is a `Deltas` slice \u2014 a list of `Delta{Type, Object}` structs for the same key, allowing multiple events to accumulate if the consumer falls behind.\n\nThe five delta types (`delta_fifo.go:170-193`) and their semantics:\n\n| Type | Meaning |\n|---|---|\n| `Added` | Object appeared for the first time |\n| `Updated` | Object changed |\n| `Deleted` | Object was removed (or final state was `DeletedFinalStateUnknown`) |\n| `Replaced` | Emitted on relist; object state is a snapshot (may or may not have changed) |\n| `Sync` | Periodic resync event; same object state, forces re-delivery to handlers |\n\n**Tombstones:** When the watch stream is interrupted and a relist finds an object missing from the server that was previously present, DeltaFIFO cannot deliver the final state of the object (it was deleted while disconnected). It synthesizes a `Deleted` delta wrapping a `DeletedFinalStateUnknown{Key, Obj}` (`delta_fifo.go:742`), where `Obj` is the last-known cached state. Controllers that implement `OnDelete` handlers must use `cache.DeletionHandlingMetaNamespaceKeyFunc` or pattern-match on `DeletedFinalStateUnknown` to extract the key safely.\n\n**Transformer:** The `TransformFunc` hook (`delta_fifo.go:164`) \u2014 configured via `SharedIndexInformer.SetTransform()` (`shared_informer.go:521`) \u2014 allows callers to strip or modify fields before the object is stored in the cache. The canonical use case is stripping `managedFields` from objects to reduce memory consumption. Because the transformed object is stored in the cache and returned via listers, the transformation must be stable and idempotent.\n\n### 3.3 SharedIndexInformer (`tools/cache/shared_informer.go`)\n\n`sharedIndexInformer` (`shared_informer.go:420`) is the central coordinator. Its internal structure:\n\n```\nsharedIndexInformer\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 controller (tools/cache.Controller)         // wraps Reflector + DeltaFIFO\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 indexer (Indexer / ThreadSafeStore)         // the local object cache\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 processor (sharedProcessor)                 // fan-out to registered handlers\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 cacheMutationDetector (MutationDetector)    // debug tool\n\u2514\u2500\u2500 transform (TransformFunc)                   // optional field stripper\n```\n\nWhen `Run()` is called, the informer:\n1. Creates a new `DeltaFIFO` backed by the `indexer`.\n2. Creates an internal `Controller` that runs the `Reflector` and calls `HandleDeltas()` / `HandleBatchDeltas()` as the pop callback.\n3. Starts the `sharedProcessor` goroutine that delivers notifications to registered handlers.\n\n**Delta dispatch path:**\n```\nReflector.watch() \u2192 DeltaFIFO.Add/Update/Delete/Replace\n\u2192 DeltaFIFO.Pop() \u2192 HandleDeltas() [locked on blockDeltas]\n  \u2192 processDeltas() \u2192 indexer.Add/Update/Delete\n  \u2192 sharedIndexInformer.OnAdd/OnUpdate/OnDelete\n    \u2192 processor.distribute(notification, isSync)\n      \u2192 processorListener.add(notification) [per-handler goroutine]\n        \u2192 handler.OnAdd / OnUpdate / OnDelete\n```\n\n`blockDeltas` (`shared_informer.go:451`) is a mutex that prevents late-joining event handlers from racing with active delta dispatch. When `AddEventHandler()` is called after `Run()`, the informer acquires `blockDeltas` while feeding the new handler a synthetic backlog of all currently-cached objects as `Added` events, then releases the lock to resume normal dispatch. This ensures the new handler sees a consistent snapshot before receiving incremental updates.\n\n**Resync:** `Sync` deltas are generated by the resync timer. The `sharedProcessor` tracks which handlers requested a resync period shorter than the informer's default, and only routes `isSync=true` notifications to those handlers. This allows multiple controllers sharing one informer to have independent resync schedules.\n\n**InOrderInformers feature gate:** Introduced at beta in v1.33 (GA default on), this gate (`features/known_features.go:66`) changes the delta delivery order guarantee. When enabled, events for a given object are delivered in arrival order, preventing a fast-followed create+delete from appearing as a spurious update. `InOrderInformersBatchProcess` (v1.35 beta default on) further batches multiple events from the same pop into a single handler call.\n\n### 3.4 SharedInformerFactory (`informers/factory.go`)\n\n`sharedInformerFactory` manages a registry of `SharedIndexInformer` instances, deduplicated by `reflect.Type`. A single informer is shared across all controllers in a process that need the same resource type, so there is at most one LIST/WATCH connection per resource type per process.\n\nKey operations:\n- `InformerFor()` (`factory.go:200`) \u2014 retrieves or lazily creates an informer for a given object type, calling the provided `NewInformerFunc` on first access.\n- `Start()` (`factory.go:144`) \u2014 launches each registered informer in a goroutine (idempotent; safe to call multiple times).\n- `WaitForCacheSync()` (`factory.go:177`) \u2014 blocks until every started informer's `HasSynced()` returns true, i.e., the initial list has been processed.\n\n### 3.5 Work Queue (`util/workqueue/`)\n\nThe work queue is the boundary between the informer's event-driven notification path and the controller's reconciliation logic. The interface hierarchy:\n\n```\nTypedInterface[T]\n  \u2514\u2500\u2500 TypedDelayingInterface[T]        // AddAfter(item, delay)\n        \u2514\u2500\u2500 TypedRateLimitingInterface[T]  // AddRateLimited, Forget, NumRequeues\n```\n\nThe standard controller pattern is:\n1. In `OnAdd`/`OnUpdate`/`OnDelete`, call `queue.Add(key)`.\n2. In the reconciliation goroutine, call `queue.Get()`, process, then call `queue.Done(key)`.\n3. On failure, call `queue.AddRateLimited(key)` to re-enqueue with backoff.\n4. On success, call `queue.Forget(key)` to reset the failure counter.\n\n**Deduplication:** The queue guarantees that an item appearing multiple times before it is picked up by a worker is delivered only once. This collapses a burst of rapid updates (e.g., label flapping) into a single reconciliation, which is one of the most important properties of the controller pattern.\n\n**Default rate limiter** (`util/workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go:51`):\n```go\nNewTypedMaxOfRateLimiter(\n    NewTypedItemExponentialFailureRateLimiter(5ms, 1000s),  // per-item exponential backoff\n    &TypedBucketRateLimiter{rate.NewLimiter(10, 100)},      // global token bucket 10 QPS / 100 burst\n)\n```\n\nThe `MaxOf` combinator returns the longer of the two delays, so a repeatedly-failing item experiences exponential backoff while overall re-enqueue throughput is bounded by the token bucket.\n\n**Delaying queue** (`util/workqueue/delaying_queue.go`): Uses a min-heap sorted by fire time. A background goroutine drains items whose delay has elapsed into the base queue. This supports patterns like \"requeue in 30 seconds to check external state.\"\n\n### 3.6 Leader Election (`tools/leaderelection/`)\n\n`LeaderElector` (`leaderelection.go:188`) implements the distributed leader election protocol required for HA controller deployments. The lock backend is abstracted by `resourcelock.Interface` (`tools/leaderelection/resourcelock/interface.go:81`); the only currently supported backend is `LeaseLock` backed by a `coordination.k8s.io/v1 Lease` object (endpoints and configmap locks were removed in earlier versions).\n\nThe three timing parameters and their constraints (`leaderelection.go:77-88`):\n- `LeaseDuration` > `RenewDeadline` > `JitterFactor * RetryPeriod`\n\nA candidate that holds the lease renews it every `RetryPeriod` within a `RenewDeadline` window. A candidate that observes no renewal for a full `LeaseDuration` considers the current holder dead and attempts to acquire. The ARCHITECTURE.md notes: \"if you want an 8x margin of safety, set LeaseDuration to 60s and RenewDeadline to 30s.\" Tightening these values reduces failover latency but increases API server load and false-positives in high-latency networks.\n\n`LeaderCallbacks` (`leaderelection.go:173`) provides three hooks:\n- `OnStartedLeading(ctx)` \u2014 called when this instance acquires the lease; ctx is cancelled when the lease is lost.\n- `OnStoppedLeading()` \u2014 called after `OnStartedLeading` returns; the controller must exit or restart here.\n- `OnNewLeader(identity)` \u2014 informational, called whenever the observed leader identity changes.\n\n---\n\n## 4. End-to-End Request and Control Flow\n\n### 4.1 GET request flow\n\n```\nUser code\n  \u2192 cs.CoreV1().Pods(\"ns\").Get(ctx, \"foo\", metav1.GetOptions{})\n  \u2192 generated typed client builds a rest.Request\n  \u2192 Request.Do(ctx)\n     \u2192 flowcontrol.RateLimiter.Accept()  // client-side token bucket wait\n     \u2192 http.Client.Do(req)\n        \u2192 RoundTripper chain:\n             ImpersonatingRoundTripper \u2192 UserAgentRoundTripper \u2192 BearerAuthRoundTripper\n             \u2192 TLS transport \u2192 kube-apiserver\n        \u2190 HTTP response\n     \u2192 Result.Into(&pod)   // deserialize JSON/Protobuf into typed struct\n  \u2190 *v1.Pod\n```\n\n### 4.2 Controller reconciliation flow\n\n```\nkube-apiserver \u2500\u2500LIST/WATCH\u2500\u2500\u25ba Reflector\n                                 \u2502 (per-page, 500 items default)\n                                 \u25bc\n                             DeltaFIFO.Add/Update/Delete/Replace\n                                 \u2502\n                                 \u25bc (pop goroutine)\n                             sharedIndexInformer.HandleDeltas()\n                                 \u2502\n                          \u250c\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2534\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2510\n                          \u25bc                 \u25bc\n                     indexer.Update    sharedProcessor.distribute()\n                     (ThreadSafeStore)      \u2502\n                                           \u25bc (per-handler goroutine)\n                                      ResourceEventHandler.OnAdd/OnUpdate/OnDelete\n                                           \u2502\n                                           \u25bc (controller code)\n                                      queue.Add(key)\n                                           \u2502\n                                     worker goroutine\n                                           \u2502\n                                           \u25bc\n                                      queue.Get()\n                                      lister.Get(key)     // reads from indexer, no API call\n                                      reconcile(obj)\n                                      queue.Done(key)\n                                      // on error: queue.AddRateLimited(key)\n                                      // on success: queue.Forget(key)\n```\n\nThe critical property is that the worker reads from the local cache via the lister (no API call), and only writes back to the API server when reconciliation requires a mutation. Under normal operation, the only outbound API traffic from a controller is the initial LIST/WATCH setup and write operations.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 5.1 Watch reconnect and relist\n\nWhen the Reflector's watch stream ends (network error, server-side timeout, `410 Gone`), it re-executes `ListAndWatchWithContext` via `BackoffUntil`. A full relist from `resourceVersion: \"\"` is triggered when the last known `resourceVersion` is too old for the API server's watch cache. During relist, the `DeltaFIFO.Replace()` call computes which previously-known objects are absent in the new list and synthesizes `Deleted` deltas for them (`delta_fifo.go:560-632`). This makes the mechanism self-healing, but relist imposes a spike of LIST load on the API server and memory pressure on the client, proportional to the size of the resource set.\n\nWatch bookmarks (`AllowWatchBookmarks: true`) reduce the relist frequency by giving the Reflector a more recent `resourceVersion` to resume from, even during quiet periods with no real events.\n\n### 5.2 Tombstones and missed deletes\n\nA controller that does not handle `DeletedFinalStateUnknown` will behave incorrectly when the cluster has high object churn and the watch buffer overflows (HTTP 410). The stale `Obj` inside the tombstone is the last known state, not the final state at delete time. Controllers should always use `cache.DeletionHandlingMetaNamespaceKeyFunc` or explicitly type-assert against `cache.DeletedFinalStateUnknown` in their delete handlers.\n\n### 5.3 Cache mutation hazard\n\nObjects returned by the indexer are pointers into the shared cache. Mutating a retrieved object directly corrupts the cache state for all other readers. The `KUBE_CACHE_MUTATION_DETECTOR` environment variable activates `defaultCacheMutationDetector` (`tools/cache/mutation_detector.go:55`), which takes periodic deep-copy snapshots and panics if cached objects change between snapshots. This detector is expensive (memory leak warning is printed on enable) and intended only for debugging. In production, callers must always deep-copy before mutating: `obj.DeepCopy()`.\n\n### 5.4 Client-side rate limiting: tuning and risks\n\nThe default `QPS=5.0, Burst=10` is appropriate for light workloads but will cause controllers watching thousands of objects or performing many writes to queue behind the token bucket. Symptoms: `longThrottleLatency` log entries (threshold 50ms, `rest/request.go:54`). The fix is to increase `QPS` and `Burst` in `rest.Config`. Setting them too high increases API server load and may trigger server-side API Priority and Fairness rate limiting.\n\nEach typed client call blocks in `flowcontrol.RateLimiter.Accept()` (`util/flowcontrol/throttle.go:125`) before making the HTTP request, so rate limiting adds latency on the client goroutine. Controllers with high write rates should use batched writes or server-side apply with field manager scoping.\n\n### 5.5 SharedInformerFactory and informer sharing\n\nBecause `InformerFor()` deduplicates by `reflect.Type`, all controllers using the same `SharedInformerFactory` for the same resource type share one LIST/WATCH connection. Adding more controllers does not add more API server load for watches. However, if two controllers need the same resource with different field selectors (e.g., pods on different nodes), they must use separate `ListWatch` instances and separate informers, or they will observe all objects. There is no built-in support for per-field-selector informer deduplication.\n\n### 5.6 Leader election split-brain window\n\nLeader election is based on a Lease object in etcd. After a leader fails, the new leader cannot take over until `LeaseDuration` elapses. During this window the cluster is running without the controller. Reducing `LeaseDuration` shortens this window but increases the risk of false-positive leader eviction when network jitter causes a renewal to be briefly delayed. A leader that loses the lock (renewal fails within `RenewDeadline`) will have `OnStoppedLeading()` called; if the controller does not exit promptly, two leaders may act concurrently until one gives up.\n\n### 5.7 Handler backpressure\n\n`sharedProcessor.distribute()` (`shared_informer.go:872`) sends notifications to each `processorListener` via a buffered channel. If a handler is slow, the buffer fills and the distributor blocks \u2014 stalling delta delivery for all handlers sharing that informer. The ARCHITECTURE.md explicitly states: \"a client must process each notification promptly; lengthy processing should be passed off to something else, for example through a `client-go/util/workqueue`.\" Blocking in an event handler is a latent reliability bug.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Extension Points, Customization Hooks, and Risks\n\n### 6.1 Transport wrapping (`transport.Config.WrapTransport`)\n\n`WrapTransport` (`transport/config.go:68`) is a `WrapperFunc` that receives the base `http.RoundTripper` after TLS setup and before the built-in auth wrappers. It is the correct place to inject custom middleware (request tracing, custom headers, metrics). Incorrect implementations that fail to forward the request or that hold the connection open can silently break all API communication. The `DebugWrappers` round tripper (`transport/round_trippers.go:73`) is an example of the pattern.\n\n### 6.2 ResourceEventHandler / FilteringResourceEventHandler\n\n`ResourceEventHandler` (`tools/cache/controller.go:264`) is the primary extension point for controllers. `FilteringResourceEventHandler` (`controller.go:339`) wraps a handler with a predicate function, allowing controllers to receive only objects matching certain criteria without forking the informer. The predicate runs on the informer's delivery goroutine, so it must be fast and must not block.\n\n### 6.3 TransformFunc (cache object transformation)\n\n`SetTransform(TransformFunc)` (`shared_informer.go:521`) registers a function called by DeltaFIFO before storing an object in the cache. Use it to strip expensive or sensitive fields (e.g., `managedFields`, large annotations). Risk: the transform is applied before the object enters the cache, so listers return the transformed object. Code that expects full field presence will break silently. The transform must be idempotent because the reflector may re-deliver the same object during resync.\n\n### 6.4 Custom IndexFuncs\n\n`Indexer.AddIndexers(Indexers)` allows controllers to add secondary indexes to the cache. The built-in `NamespaceIndex` maps namespace to object keys. Custom indexes (e.g., by ownerReference UID, by label value) enable O(1) lookups that would otherwise require a full cache scan. `AddIndexers` must be called before `Start()` (`shared_informer.go:624`); adding indexes to a running informer panics. Risk: each index trades memory for lookup speed; large object sets with many custom indexes can accumulate significant heap.\n\n### 6.5 ListerWatcher customization\n\n`NewListWatchFromClient` (`tools/cache/listwatch.go:218`) and `NewFilteredListWatchFromClient` accept option-modifier functions that allow callers to set field selectors or label selectors on the underlying LIST/WATCH requests. This is how node-local controllers (e.g., the kubelet itself when talking to the API server for its own pods) restrict their watch to a subset of objects. Incorrect field selectors can cause controllers to miss objects they should manage, leading to orphaned resources.\n\n### 6.6 WatchErrorHandler\n\n`SharedIndexInformer.SetWatchErrorHandler()` (`shared_informer.go:503`) replaces the default watch error logging. This is useful for controllers that want to distinguish transient connectivity issues from persistent errors (e.g., to trigger alerting). The handler runs in the reflector's goroutine and must not block.\n\n### 6.7 Feature Gate integration\n\nClient-go exposes its own feature gate registry (`features/features.go`) independent of the main Kubernetes feature gate registry. External programs that embed client-go can call `AddFeaturesToExistingFeatureGates()` to register client-go feature gates into their own registry, or `ReplaceFeatureGates()` to supply a fully custom gate implementation. Current gates relevant to controller authors:\n\n| Gate | Default (\u22651.35) | Effect |\n|---|---|---|\n| `WatchListClient` | true (beta) | Streaming initial sync instead of paginated LIST |\n| `InOrderInformers` | true (beta) | Ordered event delivery per object key |\n| `InOrderInformersBatchProcess` | true (beta) | Batch processing of atomic event groups |\n| `InformerResourceVersion` | true (GA) | Informer tracks and exposes its current resource version |\n| `AtomicFIFO` | true (beta) | FIFO processes items atomically |\n\n---\n\n## 7. Operational Implications\n\n### 7.1 Memory footprint\n\nThe dominant memory cost is the informer cache. Every watched object is held in `ThreadSafeStore` (`tools/cache/thread_safe_store.go`) as a Go interface pointing to a deep-copied object. A cluster with 50,000 pods, each ~10 KB of JSON, occupies ~500 MB in the cache of a controller that watches pods. Common mitigations:\n\n- **TransformFunc** to strip `managedFields`, `annotations`, or other large fields not used by the controller.\n- **Metadata-only informers** via `metadata.Client` \u2014 each `PartialObjectMetadata` is ~1 KB regardless of spec/status size, a 10x reduction for large resources.\n- **Field selectors** on the `ListerWatcher` to watch only the subset of objects the controller manages.\n\nSecondary indexes also consume memory proportional to the number of indexed values. Each entry in an `index` is `map[string]sets.Set[string]`, storing all object keys that share an indexed value.\n\n### 7.2 API server connection management\n\nEach `SharedInformerFactory` opens one long-lived watch connection per resource type. A process with 20 informers maintains 20 persistent connections. Kubernetes API Priority and Fairness (APF) assigns these to flow schemas based on user-agent and service account. In large clusters, a large number of controller processes each watching many resources can consume significant APF bandwidth. The `QPS`/`Burst` settings in `rest.Config` throttle only write/read calls, not the watch stream itself \u2014 watches are exempt from the token bucket.\n\n### 7.3 Rate limiter tuning for write-heavy controllers\n\nControllers that reconcile many objects in parallel (e.g., a node controller during a rolling upgrade) need elevated `QPS`/`Burst` settings. A practical approach is to set `QPS=20, Burst=30` as a starting point and monitor the `rest_client_rate_limiter_duration_seconds` metric (exposed if a Prometheus-compatible metrics provider is registered via `tools/metrics`). The workqueue's `DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter` provides exponential per-item backoff starting at 5ms and capping at 1000s (`util/workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go:51`), which is appropriate for most controllers.\n\n### 7.4 Reflector metrics for cache observability\n\nThe `MetricsProvider` interface (`tools/cache/reflector_metrics.go:50`) allows registering counters and summaries for:\n- `NewListsMetric` \u2014 counts LIST calls (spikes indicate relist storms)\n- `NewWatchesMetric` / `NewShortWatchesMetric` \u2014 watch count; short watches indicate connectivity instability\n- `NewWatchDurationMetric` / `NewListDurationMetric` \u2014 latency distributions\n- `NewLastResourceVersionMetric` \u2014 the current resource version (a lagging value indicates a stale cache)\n\nThe workqueue exposes analogous metrics via `MetricsProvider` (`util/workqueue/metrics.go`): queue depth, adds, latency, work duration, and retries. These are the primary operational signals for controller health.\n\n### 7.5 Graceful shutdown sequencing\n\nCorrect shutdown requires:\n1. Stop the `SharedInformerFactory` (close the stop channel or call `Shutdown()`), which stops all reflectors.\n2. Drain the work queue with `queue.ShutDownWithDrain()`, which blocks until all in-flight `Get()`-ed items have been `Done()`.\n3. If using leader election, ensure `OnStoppedLeading()` triggers a clean shutdown.\n\nSkipping queue drain can leave objects in a partially-reconciled state. Calling `ShutDown()` (not `ShutDownWithDrain()`) drops items that were `Get()`-ed but not yet `Done()`; they will not be requeued after restart unless the informer re-delivers them on relist.\n\n---\n\n## 8. File and Directory Map\n\n| Path | Role |\n|---|---|\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md` | Authoritative design narrative for contributors |\n| # client-go: Advanced Systems Guide

## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies

`client-go` is the official Go client library for the Kubernetes API. It is the canonical package for anything that communicates with kube-apiserver: controllers in the control plane, CLI tools like `kubectl`, admission webhooks, and any external operator. Almost every component in the Kubernetes ecosystem is, directly or transitively, a client-go user.

**Canonical location:** `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/` (published externally as `k8s.io/client-go`).

The library defines a strict version relationship: `client-go v0.X.Y` corresponds to Kubernetes `v1.X.Y`. A client built against an older version is guaranteed to work against a newer API server (forward compatibility), but the reverse is not guaranteed (see `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md`).

### Upstream dependencies

- `k8s.io/apimachinery` — provides the runtime object model, serialization, watch event types, and the `StatusError` type used for structured error handling.
- `k8s.io/api` — contains the generated Go types for all built-in API groups.
- `k8s.io/code-generator` — generates the typed clientsets, informers, listers, and apply-configuration builders that make up the majority of the code in `kubernetes/`, `informers/`, `listers/`, and `applyconfigurations/`.

### Downstream consumers

- **kube-controller-manager / cloud-controller-manager** — every built-in controller uses the `SharedInformerFactory`, typed clientsets, and the work-queue pattern.
- **kube-scheduler** — uses informers for its cache of pods, nodes, and other scheduling-relevant objects.
- **`kubectl`** — uses `tools/clientcmd` for kubeconfig loading and the `dynamic` client for generic resource manipulation.
